Résumé du chapitre 12 : Information Security Maintenance
Pour vous préparer à la lecture du chapitre
Si vous souhaitez avoir un aperçu du chapitre à lire ou encore si vous vous sentez moins à l’aise avec la lecture de textes en anglais, ce bref résumé vous sera utile.
Il vous présente les grandes lignes du contenu à voir cette semaine et les principales notions abordées dans votre manuel.
À elle seule, la lecture de ce résumé ne suffit pas à acquérir toutes les connaissances au programme cette semaine, mais elle constitue une bonne façon de vous préparer à l’étude du chapitre ou de le réviser.
Bonne lecture!
•	Le changement est inévitable. Par conséquent, les organisations devraient établir des procédures pour gérer le changement dans la mise en œuvre et la maintenance du programme de sécurité de l’information.
•	C’est le chef de la sécurité de l’information (CISO, pour chief information security officer) qui détermine si le programme de sécurité de l’information peut s’adapter au changement en cours d’implantation ou si c’est tout le processus du SecSDLC qui doit être repris depuis le début.
•	Le modèle de maintenance recommandé dans le manuel est basé sur les cinq domaines suivants :
o	la surveillance externe;
o	la surveillance interne;
o	la planification et l’évaluation des risques;
o	l’analyse des vulnérabilités et leur élimination;
o	la revue du programme de sécurité de l’information.
•	Pour s’assurer d’avoir en tout temps une information à jour, la communauté d’intérêts de la sécurité de l’information, dirigée par le CISO, doit constamment surveiller ces trois composantes de la sécurité : les menaces, les vulnérabilités et les actifs.
•	Pour aider l’équipe de la sécurité de l’information à gérer et à maintenir le programme de sécurité en place, l’organisation devrait adopter un modèle de gestion en sécurité de l’information. Ces modèles permettent de gérer un groupe particulier d’activités ou de fonctions d’affaires.
•	Le NIST SP 800-100 Information Security Handbook est un modèle de gestion servant à guider le responsable de la sécurité dans la gestion de la maintenance de la sécurité de l’information une fois que le programme de sécurité est opérationnel. Il préconise de surveiller les 13 domaines de sécurité suivants :
o	la gouvernance de la sécurité;
o	le cycle de développement des systèmes d’information;
o	la sensibilisation et la formation;
o	la planification et le contrôle des investissements en capital;
o	l’interconnexion des systèmes;
o	les mesures de performance;
o	la planification de la sécurité;
o	les plans de contingence;
o	la gestion du risque;
o	l’évaluation de la sécurité en fonction de la certification et de l’accréditation;
o	les services de sécurité et l’acquisition de produits;
o	la réponse aux incidents;
o	la gestion du changement.
•	Le modèle de maintenance de la sécurité complète le modèle de gestion retenu en permettant à l’organisation de concentrer ses efforts sur la maintenance des systèmes. Il comprend cinq domaines :
o	la surveillance externe;
o	la surveillance interne;
o	la planification et l’évaluation des risques;
o	l’analyse des vulnérabilités et leur élimination;
o	la revue du programme de sécurité de l’information.
•	La surveillance externe fournit des informations sur les nouvelles menaces, vulnérabilités et attaques : l’organisation peut ainsi se défendre efficacement, et ce, en temps opportun.
•	La surveillance interne permet de maintenir à jour une connaissance de l’état de la sécurité sur l’ensemble des réseaux de l’organisation, de connaître l’inventaire des composants du système et les mesures pour se défendre contre les menaces.
•	La planification et l’évaluation des risques permettent de garder le programme de sécurité de l’information à jour dans son ensemble.
•	L’analyse des vulnérabilités et leur élimination permettent de détecter les vulnérabilités, de les documenter et de les éliminer en temps opportun.
•	La revue du programme de sécurité de l’information permet de garder le programme de sécurité fonctionnel, tel qu’il a été planifié, et de l’améliorer de façon continue.Le modèle de maintenance 
Source : Whitman, Michael E. et Mattord, Herbert J. (2022). Principles of Information Security, 7e édition. Cengage, p. 470.
•	La sécurité de l’information nécessite la protection des données et des actifs. Il existe un certain nombre de politiques et d’activités de sécurité physique qui peuvent être mises en œuvre pour protéger des éléments physiques, des objets ou des zones. Il s’agit notamment des contrôles d’accès physiques, des contrôles de sécurité physique (par exemple, les clôtures, les cartes d’identité, les serrures, etc.), de la sécurité contre les incendies, de la ventilation et de la climatisation, de la gestion de l’énergie électrique et de la sécurisation des données et des appareils portables.
ADM 6046 Gestion de la sécurité des technologies de l’information ARTICLE DE LA SEMAINE 13 ARTICLE Humphreys (2008). Information Security Management Standards: Compliance, Governance and Risk Management SOMMAIRE DE L’ARTICLE L’article de E. Humphreys explore les standards de la gestion de la sécurité de l’information et les normes internationales ISO/CEI qui sont utilisées par des centaines de milliers d’organisations à travers le monde. L’auteur analyse ce que ces normes offrent aux organisations, quels en sont les avantages et de quelle façon elles peuvent contribuer à la conformité légale desdites organisations. Il examine aussi les menaces internes à la sécurité de l’information en les considérant comme un problème majeur auquel les organisations doivent faire face. L’article met également en évidence la façon dont les normes internationales ISO/CEI peuvent aider à résoudre le problème des menaces internes. Information security management standards: Compliance, governance and risk management Edward Humphreys* XiSEC, Martlesham Heath, Suffolk IP5 3TF, UK abstract Managing information security as opposed to the IT security is an area that is now eventually coming of age. For many years the focus has been mainly on IT security and with the implementation of such security left to the IT department and technical experts. Early in the 90s things started to change with the first draft of an information security management standard BS 7799 focusing in on security related to people, processes, information as well as IT. Since then there has been many developments taking us to where we are today with these early security management standards being transformed in international standards published by ISO/IEC. These standards are being used by hundreds of thousands of organisations using these standards worldwide. Based on the authors previously copyrighted writings, this article explores what these standards have got to offer organisations, what benefits are to be gained and how such standards have helped with compliance. In particular it focuses in on the insider threat as an example of one of the growing problems that organisations need to deal with and how these international standards are useful in helping to solve the insider threat problem. ª 2008 Published by Elsevier Ltd. 1. Insider threats To arrive at a definitive definition of insider threat is difficult because there are many different views on this. For the purposes of this paper the following working definition will be used: insider threats are those caused by employees, staff, management or on-site contractors that take advantage of the vulnerabilities in the systems, processes and applications: (i) for personal gain and profit such as fraud and scams, theft of information and other assets, or (ii) to engage in reckless behaviour (if not truly intending to cause harm), malicious damage and/or sabotage (if intending to cause harm) at the corporate, operational or IT level. An insider threat might involve someone at the Board of Directors level, the CEO or senior management levels. Equally it might be any employee not ranked as a management grade. It might involve technical specialists, system administrators or non-specialists. Every person in an organisation is a potential candidate if they have the motivation to take advantage of the opportunities confronting them from being and working inside the organisation. Someone might hold a grudge, be angry or disgruntled by unfair treatment. This may be a motive for the employee to sabotage an IT system, to destroy company files and information assets, to badmouth the company or their boss, to steal from the company or to take other actions to compensate for their grievance. There aremany examples of this type of problem that have resulted in major problems for organisation not only internally but also having serious implications outside of the organisation. For example, the disgruntled employee at the time of destroying company files might also be destroying information belonging to business partners, customers or suppliers. Of course it could be someone in a company with privileged access to information that might provide them with personal * Tel.: þ44 77 53804272. E-mail address: edwardj7@msn.com available at www.sciencedirect.com www.compseconline.com/publications/prodinf.htm 1363-4127/$ – see front matter ª 2008 Published by Elsevier Ltd. doi:10.1016/j.istr.2008.10.010 information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 gain if they were to use this information in a manner that was unauthorised, illegal or in any way outside of the job function. Fraud is one example of this type of threat. Surveys such as those conducted in the United States, UK and elsewhere have found that on average that 35% of security incidents occurring in organisations were caused by insider threats and this number of incidents is growing year by year. Many studies have shown the most common of insider threats involve information theft, fraud or some form of sabotage to company assets. 2. Insider vulnerabilities Although it is well recognised that organisations have a growing problem with insider threat there is the dual problem of the insider vulnerabilities. Of course these types of vulnerability turn out to threaten the security of the organisation’s information assets. For example human errors or mistakes in handling data through say careless working or lack of training is a category of vulnerability that can threaten the integrity of information assets. Also lack of awareness of the dangers of reverse social engineering attacks can result in an unsuspecting employee disclosing company confidential information. So there are many insider vulnerabilities that could, either by accident or by being exploited also be considered as an insider threat. 3. Information security – starting at the top The Board of Directors of an organisation is ultimately responsible for the protection of the company assets (Humphreys and Plate, 2008). They need to ensure correct governance of the organisation is being deployed with a risk management process in place and an effective system of internal controls in place to support this risk management process. There are many legal obligations for which the Board and its directors are responsible and there are stiff penalties for failing to meet these legal commitments and duties. Hence corporate governance should accomplish these aims in a manner essentially free of abuse and corruption, and with due diligence and proper regard for the rule of law. In terms of information security, due diligence should be undertaken to ensure risks are known and managed when it comes to dealing with company assets and their protection. This involves due diligence in identifying and assessing the business and information security risks, as well as implementing an effective system of controls, and that regular monitoring and reviews are undertaken in order to ensure the controls remain effective and if not, that appropriate improvements are made. In other words there should be an information security governance process in place in particular to protect the organisation’s information assets. 4. Organisational security From the Board of Directors and the CEO downwards through all levels of management to the general member of staff, information security should be everyone’s responsibility. At the top the Board of Directors should set the overall company policy and direction for information security, they should ensure adequate resources are available to implement information security. Senior management in turn should provide additional support and direction to enforce the Board’s directions. In turn the various business units need to engage in information security as part of their day-to-day work and job functions. This is how things should be to ensure from top to bottom, through all staff levels, information security is effectively implemented. Unfortunately security incidents occur because of flaws, gaps or vulnerabilities somewhere in this management framework and chain of policy, direction and implementation. There is no excuse as there are well proven ways of solving most of these problems which have been tried and tested for many years. Organisations have engaged in applying best practice information security management for decades and today much of this is enshrined in many of the international standards that have been developed such as ISO/IEC 27001 and ISO/IEC 27002. 5. ISMS standards The ISO/IEC 27001 standard provides a specification for an information security management system (ISMS). This standard is used throughout the world by organisations, both commercial and government, as the basis for the management of the organisation’s policy and implementation of information security. It is being used by small, medium and large organisations across a diverse range of business sectors. In fact the standard is designed to be flexible enough to be used by all types of organisation. The standard has become the de facto ‘‘common-language’’ for information security management. The ISO/IEC 27001 ISMS standard adopts the well-known PDCA process approach as illustrated in Fig. 1. The PDCA approach is also called a continuous improvement since the management system is regularly monitored and reviewed to check whether the controls to manage the risks are still effective and if they are not, then improved controls need to be implemented. One of the standards that support the implementation of ISO/IEC 27001 is the code of practice ISO/IEC 27002. This code of practice provides implementation guidance for the information security controls defined in ISO/IEC 27001 (Annex A). The control areas covered by both these standards are illustrated in Fig. 2. These areas of controls provide a comprehensive coverage of organisational requirements for managing risk across the business involving people, information, processes, services, IT and physical assets. 6. ISO/IEC 27001 processes (Humphreys, 2007) ISO/IEC 27001 contains both processes built into the PDCA model (see Fig. 1) and controls (see Fig. 2) both of which in combination can be used for dealing with insider threats. One 248 information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 of the processes that are essential in understanding and combating information security incidents and compromises is having an effective risk management process in place. 6.1. ISMS risk management As was mentioned earlier in this article, risk management is key to the governance of the organisation and to the protection of its information assets. If an organisation does not know the risks it faces, it will not be able to implement proper and effective protection. The risk process is shown in Fig. 3. Assessing the risks (Humphreys, 2007) involves identifying the assets, the threats and the vulnerabilities, and the calculating the: Risk of exposure ¼ likelihood of an insider threat exploiting the vulnerability/vulnerabilities of one or more organisations assets; Level of risk ¼ (risk of exposure) (the consequences of this risk taking effect i.e. the impact on the organisation). The impact may be a financial loss, theft or damage to information, loss of image and reputation, legal action and so on. Once the risks have been identified, a risk register should be compiled which records these risks in order of their severity as well details of the type of risk, the estimate impact and other details that will be useful to build up a risk profile of the organisation. In order to support ISO/IEC 27001 a set of guidelines have been produced and published as ISO/IEC 27005 giving more details of the concepts and methods behind the risk assessment process. The major insider threat can be classified as fraud, theft and sabotage (Fig. 4). The treatment of risks involves deciding how the risks that the organisation has identified should be dealt with. ISO/IEC 27001 gives four treatment options: Knowingly and objectively accept/retain the risk. Avoiding the risk by modifying or avoiding the planned activity that causes the risk. Fig. 1 – ISMS PDCA model. Fig. 2 – Areas of Information security control. Fig. 3 – Risk management process. Fig. 4 – Major insider threat categories. information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 249 Transfer the risk by insurance or by contracting out the risk. Reduce the risk by implementing a system of internal controls. This step of the process involves a management decisionmaking activity (Humphreys, 2007) to decide the best treatment option(s) to take. Management need to look at the costs and benefits of implementing controls or take action to mitigate the risks versus costs of not implementing controls or taking action. This management decision-making activity carries forward to selection of specific controls. A general principle is to spend just enough on information security – not too little or not too much as there is a point beyond which the more money we plough into information security we get diminishing returns for the organisation’s investments. It is a case of minimising the risks and maximising the organisation’s investment in security. Whatever the organisation does to manage the risk there will always be a residual risk that can never be reduced to zero. The more money is spent trying to reduce the residual risk beyond a certain point, the more likely it is that an organisation will be chasing rapidly diminishing returns for no real gain. Once the decision has been made as to which controls need to be implemented then the organisation needs to implement these controls and use them in practice. This should include any necessary training for staff in the use of these controls and the allocation of roles and responsibilities with those that have been given specific security-related duties. The next step in the risk management process is to monitor and review the risks on a regular basis. This is necessary since day-to-day changes will happen that could alter the risk profile of the organisation. Hence it is necessary to monitor changes and reassess the risks. If necessary, improvements might need to be made to ensure that the protection provided by the system of controls still remains effective. 6.2. ISMS measurement process It is very important that an organisation is able to check how effective its information securitymanagement is in practice. In order to enable this to happen, the organisation needs to establish a measurement process to be able to measure and assess how effective the controls are at protecting their information assets. An example might be to measure how good the controls are that have been implemented to avoid insider threats, such as the effectiveness of the access controlmethods that are being used, separation of duties, monitoring the misuse of system resources or user awareness and reporting of events and incidents that might be linked to insider activities that might compromise policies or procedures. It is imperative that the organisation is continually alert to the changes in its business environment that might have an impact on its success, governance and financial stability. Changes can easily result in fluctuations in the risk profile and the ability of the organisation to protect its information assets. Therefore to avoid the various threats that the organisation might be confronted with, such as an insider threat, it should be observing, monitoring and reviewing these changes and what influence these have on their risk profile. 6.3. ISMS incident handling Allowing an insider threat to exploit an organisational vulnerability results in an information security incident. It is very important that an organisation has a comprehensive incident handling management process in place. This is one of the requirements of ISO/IEC 27001. This process involves: Identification, detection and reporting incidents. Analysis and evaluation of incident. Responding to the incident. Recovery and cleanup of the incident. Learning from the incident. Incident handling involves taking corrective action to resolve and recover from the incidents and their reoccurrence in the future. This type of action is a reactive approach to resolve yesterday’s problems as opposed to preventive actions that is a proactive approach to avoiding occurrence of incidents in the future. An incident handling process that has been running for sometime can be a valuable source of historical data which can be used for trend analysis. This provides opportunities for improvement to ensure future availability and continuity of the organisation’s information systems rather than recovering from yesterday’s disasters and failures. Incidents occur because the organisation has provided the necessary conditions for threats, such as insider threats, to take control of the organisation’s systems by exploiting its vulnerabilities. In other words the organisation has failed in its governance and risk management duties by allowing the right conditions to be present and provide an open door to the insider threat. In addition to the incident handling requirements in ISO/ IEC 27001 and the implementation advice in ISO/IEC 27002 there is another ISO/IEC standard ISO/IEC 18044 (currently being revised and to be renumbered as ISO/IEC 27035) that provides more detailed advice on this management process. 6.4. Monitor and review Another important and critical process defined in ISO/IEC 27001 is that of monitoring and reviewing the information security management system. There are several things that could be monitored and reviewed such as: Records of the use of systems and network services. Risk reassessment reports. Use of procedures by staff. Incident handling reports. Results of measurements. Customer, service providers and employee feedback such as complaints, faults, security weaknesses or suggestions for improvement. Server audit trails. Firewall and intrusion detection device records and audit trails. Web site monitoring records. System and network configuration files. Internal audit activities and reports. 250 information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 When considering the monitoring of employees’ use of email and web site access, the organisation needs to check whether there is any applicable legislation dealing with this situation. For example, in some countries there is legislation on monitoring of employee activities. In some countries such monitoring is possible if the employee is fully aware that such controls are in place and may be used by the organisation when appropriate. One particular way of dealing with this is to get employees to sign an ‘‘Acceptable Use Policy’’ which includes provision for check compliance with this policy through use of monitoring methods. Such a policy outlines what an employee can and cannot do regarding the use of the organisations IT facilities, email system and network services, as well as what the consequences will be if the employee fails to comply. 6.5. Information security audits Audits can be carried out by internal auditors and by external auditors. ISO/IEC 27001 states there shall be an ISMS internal audit function in place which reflects the normal audit function an organisation is expect to carry out. In addition to this ISMS internal audit, the organisation might decide to have an external certification audit to have a third party check on their compliance with ISO/IEC 27001. This form of audit is carried out in the same way as ISO 9001 audits for quality, ISO 14001 audits for environment and ISO 22000 for food safety are carried out. Deciding on a third party audit is entirely up to the organisation, whereas the internal audit is an essential part of the overall process of governance, risk management and ensuring effective information security is established, implemented, monitored and reviewed, maintained and updated as a continual cycle of continual improvement process. Currently over 4600 organisations have been certified to ISO/ IEC 27001 (see www.iso27001certificates.com for more details). 6.6. Improvement process The outcome of the monitoring and review exercise, that should be a regular activity, could be recommendations for improvements to the ISMS. This could be the implementation of new controls or the modification of existing controls. These recommendations for improvements are generally the result of a change in the risk profile of the organisation. ISO/IEC 27001 is a security process standard that is driven by the notion of achieving effective information security through a cycle of continual improvement. 7. ISO/IEC 27001 controls (Humphreys, 2007) Let us now consider which of the controls in ISO/IEC 27001 should form a management baseline for dealing with insider threats. Of course there are other more specific controls we can implement from ISO/IEC 27001 depending on the nature of the organisation and its operations. 7.1. Human resources (IoD) The Human Resource department should have clearly defined procedures as regards: Recruitment of new staff, including contractors, temporary staff and cleaning staff. B Criminal background investigations. B Credit checks. B Previous employment verification. B Checks on references. B Verifying employee qualifications. During employment: B Terms and conditions, non-disclosure agreements as well as requiring all staff to sign a document stating they have read and understood the information security policies. B Periodically repeating checks on employees in highly privileged positions. B Disciplinary process. B Establish an anonymous fraud, waste and abuse reporting or whistle-blowing process (many insider crimes were observed, noticed or suspected by other employees). B Ensure appropriate segregation of duties. B Adequate awareness and training programmes are in place and used. B Initiate an early warning system to alert staff responsible for information security when an employee is identified as troubled or disgruntled. B Review on regular basis whether access rights are still valid. Terminating of employment. B Cancelling all computer accounts. B Return of company assets. B Immediate gardening leave for those highly privileged positions. 7.2. Security awareness programme User awareness is essential to good business practice. All users should be aware of the risks related to the organisation’s information systems. They also need to be familiar with the organisation’s security policies and procedures and be able to use these in their day-to-day job function. The organisation should establish a comprehensive information security awareness and training. Also regular awareness briefings, newsletters and circulars should also be in place to keep employees up to date with the latest developments. The organisation should review and update its awareness programme as and when necessary. 7.3. Information handling Controls in place to protect the organisation’s information assets should at least include the following from ISO/IEC 27001: Asset inventory. Asset classification scheme. Information handling procedures. Policy on the acceptable use of information assets. Access control policy and methods of control. Monitoring and reviewing the information handling protection arrangements making appropriate improvements as and when necessary. User awareness. information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 251 In addition to what needs to be done in ISO/IEC 27001 and the implementation guidance given in ISO/IEC 27002, there are several publications that can be used to provide more guidance (BERR). 7.4. Access control (Humphreys, 2007) Access should be given to staff based upon the person’s job role and what information they need-to-know and access to perform their duties. For example, those dealing with accounting and payroll should have access to information relating to their work in this capacity that most other employees should not have access to. There could be many different types of information an organisation has that are either sensitive or critical. This type of information requires information to be accessed on a need-to-know or need-tohave-access basis. Examples of this type of information include financial, personnel, company secrets, research results, development plans, and customer information. All access requests and rights should be formally documented and approved by whoever has the authority to grant such access rights. There are several objects of access: information, applications, operating systems, network services and physical assets including buildings, offices and equipment. Consideration should taken regarding IT roles such as developers, system and application administrators, etc. For access to sensitive systems, require approval of a data owner as well. Configure building access cards to restrict personnel to the areas and time periods required in performance of their duties. Each quarter ask managers to formally sign-off on the privileges of their direct reports. Employees who move to new positions in an organisation should have their access rights reviewed to ensure they do not retain privileges and rights beyond what they need to carry out their new job function. 7.5. Separation of duties Two-person integrity controls and/or separation (sometimes referred to as segregation) of duties are often used to add further access protection against the insider threat. The accounting profession have used and invested in these techniques for many years. Separation of duty, as a security principle, has as its primary objective the prevention of fraud and errors. This objective is achieved by disseminating the tasks and associated privileges for a specific business process among multiple users. This principle is demonstrated in the traditional example of separation of duty found in the requirement of two signatures on a check (Botha and Eloff) So, for example, an employee who has the responsibility for creating an account or issuing purchase orders should not be the same the person who can make payments. The person who develops or implements a system should not be the person who carries out an audit or review of the system. The three basic functions that need to be separate are: Authorisation functions. Recording function, e.g. preparing source documents, developing code or producing performance or audit reports. The role of those who have custody of asset whether directly or indirectly, e.g. receiving checks in mail or implementing source code or making database changes. An example of where separation of duties can be found is with those large IT organisations in order that no single employee is in a position to introduce fraudulent, malicious code or data without being detected. One of the best practice controls is to ensure that any IT system, software and information/data changes shall require that the same person perform only one of the following roles: Identification of a change request or a new requirement. Authorization and approval of the change. Design and development of an IT system, software or information system change. Implementation of a IT system, software or data/information changes. Review, inspection and testing of the IT system, software or information/data change. Implementation of separation of duties in information systems needs to consider: The process used to ensure a person’s authorization rights take account of their job role or function in the organisation. An appropriate authentication method needs to be used, such as a strong password, key or token based system, or a biometric system, or a combination of these. Specific controls such as monitoring and/or a review of an activity log should be used to address a compromise of an information system which, for example, can occur through database administration access, user administration access, or software tools which provide back-door access or supplier installed user accounts. 7.6. Administrators System and network administrators have extensive control over IT, as well as related applications, services and communicating facilities. This gives them many opportunities to carry out an insider attack. The right of accountability and control needs to be in place to avoid the organisational information systems and the information itself being compromised. Of the several controls that could be deployed, the following are some of those recommended by ISO/IEC 27001: Proper configuration of administrator accounts uniquely linked to individual administers. No default mode accounts. Adequate access controls to ensure proper login activities take place, proper control over root-level privileges and commands. Proper allocation and management of rights and privileges for administrators with appropriate reviewing procedures for removing, revoking or updating these rights. Encrypt databases to prevent administrators with access to back-ups viewing sensitive data. 252 information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 7.7. Back-ups Back-ups, especially those that are not managed properly, are critical points of weakness for the insider to exploit. Disgruntled or unhappy employees have been known to blackmail their employer by corrupting critical data and waiting for the change to spread through off-site backup rotation. Back-ups, if not properly protected by effective management controls and procedures as well as technical controls, are a good target for insiders that want to carry out a sabotage attack. One additional precaution is to take back-ups of workstations to provide a record of employee activity, in particular of an employee who might be suspected of being an insider threat. In considering this option the organisation needs to check whether there is any applicable legislation dealing with this situation. For example, in some countries there is legislation on monitoring of employee activities. Another best practice is to encrypt backup tapes and use e-vaulting of data to keep sensitive information confidential while off-site to help in minimising the insider threat. Of course the organisation should be mindful of the key management problem whether it be for this application or other situations where the use of encryption and digital signatures might prove appropriate. It has been known for a disgruntled employee who is about to be dismissed, who has access to the key management process or maybe who is in charge of the key management process, to encrypt large volumes of data and then destroy all the keys before leaving the company. This leaves the company with a big problem of lost and unrecoverable data. Again another case where segregation of duties would have helped to avoid this problem. 7.8. Mobile devices Mobile devices such as laptops, phones, USB sticks, MP3 players and other technologies that make it easy used to store large amounts of sensitive information makes it easier for the staff to remove this information off-site for their own use. This is a sure route for information theft and for an insider to collaborate with an external user and engage in a combined attack against the organisation. The insider might sell the information onto another company hoping to avoid being tracked down using the Internet to pass the information on. The insider might possibly have his own business, which he is building up and intends to use the organisation’s information for his own business needs. Again ISO/IEC provides various recommendations that can help to minimise the risks of using mobile devices both inside and outside the organisation. 7.9. Social engineering One definition of social engineering is the art of manipulating people into performing actions or divulging confidential information (Mitnick, 2004). This type of attack is similar to a face-to-face confidence trick or a simple fraud that we have been familiar with for many years. However, the term has been particularly used to mean the trickery used for obtaining information, for on-line scams or computer system access where in general the attacker may never come face-to-face with its victim. A classic example is the insider who worked for a vendor of credit card point-of-sale terminals and used social engineering techniques to obtain information via the telephone from the credit card company help desk staff. The insider with fabricated identity posed as a distraught person working for a particular, authorized merchant needing help with a malfunctioning terminal. The insider was then able to credit his own credit card by reprogramming a terminal using the information he had obtained. Many social engineering attacks are technically unsophisticated attacks. This means that organisations need to look beyond IT security to take account of a more holistic approach to information security as advocated by ISO/IEC 27001. Information security is a set of issues relating to people, business processes, legal and regulatory aspects as well as IT aspects and therefore it is important to consider interplay and interdependence between these different aspects. Here are some examples for types of attack: Pretexting – the act of creating and using an invented scenario (the pretext) to persuade a targeted victim to release information or perform an action and is typically done over the telephone. It is more than a simple lie as it most often involves some prior research or set up and the use of pieces of known information (e.g. for impersonation of the target/victim: date of birth, social security number, last telephone/electricity/as bill amount) to establish legitimacy of the caller in the mind of the victim (FTC). Phishing – the process of attempting to acquire sensitive information by fraudulent means such as gaining usernames, passwords and credit card details, by masquerading as a trustworthy entity in an electronic communication or over the phone. Vishing – the criminal activity of using a combination of phishing and voice to get social engineering and Voice over IP (VoIP). What can be done about these types of attack? Some recommendations that can be deployed from ISO/IEC 27001 are: Raise awareness with staff of the techniques and methods used by social engineers. Make sure there are effective procedures for helping the staff to deal these types of attack and to ensure that controls and processes are in place to protect sensitive and valuable information assets. Make sure there are good channels for reporting events that may turn out to be a social engineering incident and there is an appropriate escalation path for dealing with such incidents. 8. Business sector requirements (Humphreys, 2007) Every commercial sector can be plagued with the issue of insider threat, as well as the government and public sector. Some insiders work alone for personal gains and motives whilst others operate as network such as with the recent spate of cyber-espionage attacks. information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 253 Those sectors dealing with critical infrastructure such as healthcare, transportation, telecommunications, finance, food supply, utilities, public services and others are all at risk from IT sabotage. The fact that IT sabotage could be driven by an insider threat is a great concern. The insider might have a political motive, terroristmotive or may be a malcontent or in a group of malcontents operating inside and outside the organisation. Institutions and organisations belonging to the finance sector are particularly concerned with the insider threat of fraud but of course this is a problem for any company albeit the stakes are generally not quite as high. Separation/segregation of duties is an important control to prevent end-users of key financial systems from modifying the system, or accessing the underlying financial information directly. The government sector has many areas of its business where insiders potentially have the opportunity to drive an insider threat. An employee working in the local branch office of a government department who is perhaps generating fraudulent documents for immigrants who do not have the required forms of identification to obtain services, benefits, car licences, work permits legally. The insider threat may result in identity theft, illegal payments being made to citizens, immigrants or contractors, or even selling sensitive information to the media, to companies or other external parties. On the one hand the insider may be involved in fraud as a result of corruption. The insider may be working on the inside for some external organisation. The culture and working environment of an organisation can create the right or wrong conditions for a harmonious, trustworthy workforce. If the organisation does not create the right conditions for its workforce so each individual feels safe, secure and as part of a team then the organisation may either turn insiders into an insider threat or provide the right opportunities for existing insider threats to flourish. For example, employees may have: Individual personal problems that need to be dealt with such as family medical problems, substance abuse or financial difficulties. Work related problems such as physical threats from outsiders, financial compensation issues, promotional issues, an unpleasant or hostile work environment, be the subject of coercion problems with their manager or supervisor, or of the threat of layoffs or dismissal; Personal ambitions driven by greed, ideological motives, criminal motives, self-gratification/importance, excitement or the ego. All of these relate to the people aspect of information security. The people aspect is affected by many factors including: how employees are treated and managed, and supported by the human resources department and management, what job functions they have, what their pay and conditions are, and their recognition as part of the overall business team and process. ISO/IEC 27001 is a standard that can be used by all these sectors and is not dependent on whether the organisation is small, medium and large sized company. It provides a flexible holistic approach to information security in the sense that it addresses people, process, legal and IT aspects. 9. Some thoughts for the future The insider threat is a problem that has been growing and is likely to continue to grow. Organisations need to be fully mindful of this situation and implement company-wide management framework to ensure that the protection of their information assets measures up to the risks they are facing. Insecurity and security posed by the insider threat are related in the sense that they are two sides of the same coin – a coin representing uncertainty. How much security we have versus how much insecurity we have is what management needs to decide upon when weighing up the pros and cons, the costs and the benefits, the risks and impacts of investing in information security (Fig. 5). In getting the right balance, every information security scheme will always have a set of residual risks associated with it; hence the scheme has its own inherent security weakness whatever we do. Management needs to have regular contact with the users of their business processes that deal with operations and/or use IT, in order to make sure the right actions are being undertaken to achieve the right balance. There is nothing to be certain of, as Benjamin Franklin once said ‘‘There only two things we can be certain of: death and paying taxes’’. Following on from this Stephen Batchelor posed the question ‘‘Since death is certain and the time of death is uncertain what should we do?’’ An organisation might estimate that the likelihood of an insider threat occurring is 75% but when it will happen is uncertain so what should they do? They could make sure: Their risk assessment is up to date. There is an effective set of controls in place. There is an effective incident management process in operational use. There is an appropriate measurement process in place. There is adequate training and awareness given to all staff. There is regular monitoring and reviewing activities taking place to check the effectiveness of their information security. Improvements are made to their set of controls as indicated by the monitor and review process. Fig. 5 – The balance between security and insecurity. 254 information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 In other words they could apply ISO/IEC 27001 to manage their risks and provide a management framework for the protection of their information assets. references Information security: protecting your business assets, BERR, Available from: www.berr.gov.uk. He is the Chair of the ISO working group responsible for the development and maintenance of all ISO standards on information security management including ISO/IEC 27001 and ISO/IEC 27002. He has been at the centre of the development of these information security management standards as well as the international development of third party certification audit standards and practices and as such is well known and accredited with the ‘‘father’’ of these Information Security Management System (ISMS) standards. information security tec hnical report 13 (2008) 247–255 255

Le contenu du cours
Au fil des semaines, vous avez parcouru l’ensemble des chapitres et des articles au programme, vous avez réalisé différents exercices et solutionné quelques études de cas, vous avez appris à tirer parti de la norme ISO 27002 en vue d’assurer la sécurité de l’information dans une organisation. Différentes méthodologies pour assurer la gestion de la sécurité de l’information vous ont été présentées. Vous avez découvert les meilleures pratiques en sécurité et pris connaissance des actions nécessaires pour assurer une protection suffisante et contrôlée de l’ensemble des actifs informationnels.
Ayant terminé toutes ces activités d’apprentissage, vous devriez maintenant être capable d’analyser l’état de la sécurité de l’information d’une entreprise et de proposer des mesures pour répondre à ses besoins de sécurité. De plus, vous devriez avoir toutes les connaissances nécessaires pour superviser la gestion de la sécurité de l’information dans une entreprise.
Ce sont là les buts du cours, et l’examen vous offrira l’occasion de démontrer vos compétences en la matière. Vous connaissez sans doute les méthodes qui vous conviennent le mieux pour vous préparer à un examen.
Le contenu de l’examen 
Précisons seulement que l’examen porte sur l’ensemble du contenu. Il comporte des questions à développement visant à vérifier votre compréhension des principaux concepts, notions et stratégies, ainsi que votre capacité à analyser une situation problématique et à proposer des solutions pour y remédier ou prévenir une situation semblable.
Nous vous incitons donc à revoir de manière active l’ensemble des chapitres et des articles et à vous exercer à répondre à des questions variées à l’aide des questions et des exercices qui vous ont été présentés au fil des semaines.
Il serait également judicieux de revoir les objectifs d’apprentissage de chaque semaine afin de vous assurer que vous les avez bel et bien atteints. Vous devez, en effet, maîtriser les principaux concepts, être capable de les expliquer et de les appliquer. Préparez-vous également à affronter divers types de scénarios qui peuvent se produire sur le marché du travail. On vous demandera par exemple :
•	de vous mettre dans la peau d’un responsable de la sécurité de l’information pour expliquer à la haute direction vos décisions en matière de gestion;
•	de répondre à des questions que l’on vous poserait en entrevue pour obtenir un poste en sécurité de l’information;
•	de conseiller l’équipe de sécurité pour qu’elle accomplisse le travail de gestion en matière de sécurité de l’information;
•	de formuler des recommandations en vue de prévenir un incident particulier;
•	etc.
Comme vous le constatez, cet examen est en lien direct avec les activités d’apprentissage que vous avez réalisées au fil des semaines.
Bonne étude et bon succès!

ADM 6046 Gestion de la sécurité des technologies de l’information ARTICLE DE LA SEMAINE 10 ARTICLE Kodaganallur (2006). Secure E-Commerce: Understanding the Public Key Cryptography Jigsaw Puzzle SOMMAIRE DE L’ARTICLE De nos jours, presque toutes les organisations utilisent Internet pour leurs communications internes et externes. Le réseau Internet est aussi le véhicule exclusif pour le commerce électronique, que ce soit avec les clients ou les partenaires d’affaires. Cependant, l’utilisation du réseau public pose des défis de sécurité puisqu’il faut assurer la confidentialité du message, son intégrité, l’authentification de l’émetteur et du récepteur et l’irrévocabilité du message. Dans cet article, l’auteur nous explique les mécanismes du chiffrement, lesquels répondent aux défis décrits précédemment. This article was downloaded by: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] On: 30 August 2010 Access details: Access Details: [subscription number 918588849] Publisher Taylor & Francis Informa Ltd Registered in England and Wales Registered Number: 1072954 Registered office: Mortimer House, 37 41 Mortimer Street, London W1T 3JH, UK Information Security Journal: A Global Perspective Publication details, including instructions for authors and subscription information: http://www.informaworld.com/smpp/title~content=t768221795 Secure E-Commerce: Understanding the Public Key Cryptography Jigsaw Puzzle Viswanathan Kodaganallur To cite this Article Kodaganallur, Viswanathan(2006) 'Secure E-Commerce: Understanding the Public Key Cryptography Jigsaw Puzzle', Information Security Journal: A Global Perspective, 14: 6, 44 — 52 To link to this Article: DOI: 10.1201/1086.1065898X/45782.14.6.20060101/91859.9 URL: http://dx.doi.org/10.1201/1086.1065898X/45782.14.6.20060101/91859.9 PLEASE SCROLL DOWN FOR ARTICLE Full terms and conditions of use: http://www.informaworld.com/terms-and-conditions-of-access.pdf This article may be used for research, teaching and private study purposes. CRYPTOGRAPHY Secure E-Commerce: Understanding the Public Key Cryptography Jigsaw Puzzle Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 Viswanathan Kodaganallur, Ph.D. T oday almost all organizations use the Internet extensively for both intra- and inter-organizational com munications. The Internet is also the exclu sive vehicle for E-commerce transactions involving customers and other business partners. This eliminates the significant infrastructure costs of private data commu nications networks and enables even small companies to participate in E-commerce. However, extensive use of public media for transferring sensitive data poses serious security challenges. One of the main weapons in overcoming these challenges is public key cryptography. Although public key cryptography is exten sively used today, relatively few people (managerial and technical) understand its essence, preferring to leave that to the spe cialists. Although the intricate technical details are best left to the specialists, a solid understanding of the broad picture is neces sary and well within the reach of everyone. This article sets out to convey just such an understanding. BASICS OF SECRET COMMUNICATIONS Perfectly secure communication is impossi ble in theory. It is always a possibility for any message to fall into the wrong hands. The practical approach is to reduce the like lihood of this happening to acceptably small levels. Nowadays encryption is the main approach to keeping communications secret. A message being communicated (plaintext) is encrypted in such a way that the encrypted message (ciphertext) can be understood only if it is decrypted. Clearly, the message is secret only to the extent that decrypting the ciphertext is difficult. A mes sage can be encrypted in several ways. A procedure to encrypt a message is called a cipher. A trivial (and not very secure) example of a cipher is one that reverses the characters in a message. The recipient of the message, if aware of how the message has been encrypted, can decrypt it by reversing the characters in the ciphertext to recreate the plaintext. Another example is a substitution cipher, which works by substituting each character VISWANATHAN KODAGANALLUR, Ph.D., is an Associate Professor of Computing and Decision Sci ences at Seton Hall University's Stillman School of Business. He earned his Ph.D. in operations man agement from the Indian Institute of Management. Dr. Kodaganallur started his working career in academia. He then switched to the software development industry where he spent a decade before returning to academia in 2001. His research interests include information systems security, object-ori ented software development, intelligent tutoring systems, and combinatorial optimization. I NFORMA T I ON SYST EMS SECUR I T Y 44 WWW. I NFOSECTODAY . COM FIGURE 1 Encryption and Decryption with Key-Based Symmetric Ciphers Shared key plaintext Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 Symmetric cipher Encryption of the plaintext message with some other character. This can be decrypted via reverse substitution by a recipient who is aware of what substitutions have been made. In this example, the cipher operates in conjunction with a secret key (the substitution scheme). The same cipher used on the same plaintext with a different key will yield different ciphertext. Single-character substitution ciphers can be easily cracked by cryptanalysts (people who break ciphers) through techniques such as frequency analysis. For example, the most frequently occurring letters in English are “e,” “t,” and “a.” By counting the fre quency of each letter in the ciphertext, a cryptanalyst can make a guess about which letters in the ciphertext probably map to these letters. Once these are established, other clues, such as which letters commonly precede and follow these letters, can be used to make further progress. More powerful ciphers, called block ciphers, encode a block of text at a time rather than just a single character. Under these, a block of text (say, 128 bits) is replaced with a different block of the same, or different, size. The number of possible transformations increases rapidly with the block size, consequently making cryptanal ysis more difficult. Although people can come up with many different methods of encoding, the need for standardization makes it necessary to adopt one or a few methods universally. On the face of it this would seem to defeat the very purpose of encrypted communication. How Shared key ciphertext Symmetric cipher Decryption ever, with something called “key-based” ciphers, the ciphertext depends not only on the encryption procedure but also on a secret key used during the encryption process. For example, suppose the plaintext is “6011 8767 3421 8875” (perhaps a credit card number). Let us suppose that some specified method X is being used for encryption. With the secret key “7463562781,” the plaintext might get encoded to “6bgs 8ys9 0ehw yhfk”; whereas, using the same method X but a dif ferent secret key, the plaintext would be encoded to something completely different. To decrypt messages encrypted using key based ciphers, one needs to know not only the encoding procedure but also the secret key value. Keeping the key hidden from all except the message recipient makes it very difficult for unauthorized parties to obtain the plaintext from the ciphertext, even if they know what kind of cipher has been used. This process is illustrated in Figure 1. An example of a key-based block cipher is DES (Data Encryption Standard), which was adopted as a standard by the U.S. gov ernment in 1977 and the financial industry in 1981. DES uses a 56-bit key. Today, DES is not considered safe enough primarily because of its small key size. Computing technology has become powerful enough for a cryptanalyst to exhaustively try all possible key combinations on a DES cipher text within a reasonable amount of time and hence to decode DES ciphertext even with out having the secret key. CRYP TOGRAPHY 45 J ANUARY / F EBRUARY 2006 Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 Prominent among the new approaches are AES (supports key sizes of 128, 192, and 256 bits), Triple DES (overcomes the limited key size by applying DES three times with two or three different keys), Rivest ciphers (RC2, RC4, RC5, and RC6), and others such as IDEA (International Data Encryption Algorithm) and CAST. All the ciphers mentioned above are called “sym metric key” ciphers because the same secret key is used for encryption and decryption. Symmetric key ciphers that are almost impossible to break are but one part of secure E-commerce. Other issues must be addressed for secure communications in today’s world, where many parties need to communicate over an insecure medium such as the Internet. Any scheme that is used must ideally meet all of the following requirements: ■Confidentiality: It should be almost impossible for anyone but the intended recipient to decipher the message. This is the most obvious aspect of secure com munications and is well addressed by the symmetric key ciphers already men tioned. ■Scalability: The approach should be con venient to use when the number of com municants and messages is large. Secret communication with symmetric keys is not scalable when used in isolation. It runs into various problems when many unacquainted parties need to communi cate secretly; as, for example, in E-com merce transactions. The biggest bottleneck with such sys tems is the distribution and management of the secret keys. Suppose there are n parties communicating with each other and all communications need to be secret; then each party has to keep track of one secret key for each of the n–1 par ties with whom it needs to communicate. An even bigger problem is that of safely distributing the keys in the first place so that the secret keys do not fall into the wrong hands. It should be possi ble to exchange keys safely over insecure 46 media; after all, it is not practical to expect that all communicating parties will meet in person to exchange keys. Furthermore, to break a cipher, a cryptan alyst needs a lot of ciphertext created using a single key. Thus, to minimize the risk of compromising secrecy, it would be necessary to periodically change the symmetric keys used. This requirement further complicates the task of secure key distribution. ■Integrity: It should be possible for the recipient to verify that the message was not tampered with in transit. This is important because an attacker might intercept a message and insert a different one in its place. A contemporary example of this would be when a hacker intercepts a transmission from an E-commerce server to a client and inserts the hacker’s own Web page in place of the server’s page. An unsuspecting user might then enter sensitive information on the hacker’s page and submit it to the hacker. ■Authentication: It should be possible for a message recipient to verify the identity of the message originator in order to avoid dealing with an imposter. This is essential because an imposter on the Web could eas ily pose as a genuine E-commerce vendor and steal sensitive information. Another related issue is that of non repudiation; a sender of a message (such as an important legal business document) should not be able to later claim to have never sent it. This goal is achieved once the identity of the sender of a message can be irrevocably established. I will now discuss the pieces of the public key cryptography jigsaw puzzle that address each of these issues. Table 1 summarizes the issues and the techniques that address each issue. Detailed descriptions of the tech niques follow. ASYMMETRIC KEY CIPHERS We have already seen that key distribution and management is a problem with symmet ric key ciphers. Whitfield Diffie and Martin I NFORMA T I ON SYST EMS SECUR I T Y WWW. I NFOSECTODAY . COM TABLE 1 Issues in Public Key Cryptography Issue Confidentiality Scalability Integrity Authentication Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 Technique Key-based ciphers (symmetric and asymmetric) Asymmetric key ciphers, digital certificates, and Secure Sockets Layer Message digests and digital signatures Digital certificates and certificate authorities Hellman of Stanford University made path breaking discoveries that gave birth to mod ern cryptography. They demonstrated for the first time that it is possible for commu nicating parties to exchange keys securely over insecure media. They also suggested the concept of asymmetric key ciphers, wherein the keys used for encryption and decryption are different. Although they suggested the concept of asymmetric key ciphers, they were unable to develop a concrete realization of the con cept — an actual cipher that would work as they suggested. That was later done by the MIT team of Ron Rivest, Adi Shamir, and Leonard Adleman, who created RSA, the first asymmetric key cipher. Another well known asymmetric key cipher is the ElGamal cipher. The two keys of an asymmetric cipher form a key pair, with one of them being the private key and the other the public key. A key pair has the following properties: ■A message encrypted with a public key can be decoded only with the matching private key. In the case of reversible asymmetric key ciphers, the converse also works — a message encrypted with a private key can be decrypted only with the matching public key. ■Given a public key, it is almost impossi ble to figure out the matching private key. By “almost impossible,” we mean that, although it is theoretically possible to figure out the private key given only the public key, in practice it would take several years of computing. As the names imply, a private key is kept completely private — no one but the owner knows this key — but the public key can be widely distributed because it does not mat ter who gets hold of it. Communication using asymmetric key ciphers is called pub lic key cryptography. For a concrete illustration of how secure communication would work under this sys tem, suppose person A wants to send a secret message to person B, and A and B already have each other’s public keys (they could have exchanged them via e-mail, for example). Further, suppose that a hacker (H) is eager to listen in on this communica tion. A would first encrypt her message using B’s public key and send the ciphertext to B. On receiving the message, B can decrypt it using his private key. Even if H sees the ciphertext, not possessing B’s pri vate key, H can do nothing useful with the data. This process is illustrated in Figure 2. B can now reply to A by encrypting the message with A’s public key, and the con versation can proceed securely even though H can potentially intercept each and every byte of the transmissions. Although this seems to be a foolproof arrangement, this alone does not prevent H from being malicious. H might be able to intercept a message from A to B and replace it with his own message properly encrypted with B’s public key (recall that B makes no effort to hide his public key). On receiving this message, B has no way of knowing that the integrity of the message has been com promised and that the message has really been sent by an imposter acting as A. Although confidentiality has not been com promised, there has been a security breach. How can B be sure of the authenticity of the sender of the message just by looking at the message? CRYP TOGRAPHY 47 J ANUARY / F EBRUARY 2006 FIGURE 2 Secret Communication with Public Keys and Asymmetric Ciphers A’s computer B’s public key plaintext Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 FIGURE 3 Asymmetric cipher Double Encryption Scheme with Public Key Cryptography B’s computer B’s private key ciphertext Asymmetric cipher plaintext p B’s public key plaintext Asymmetric cipher A’s private key ciphertext 1 B’s computer B’s private key plaintext Asymmetric cipher Asymmetric cipher ciphertext A’s public key ciphertext 1 There is a way by which confidentiality, integrity, and authentication can all be achieved with asymmetric ciphers. Let us assume as before that A and B have exchanged public keys. Now, when A wants to send a message to B, A first encrypts the message using B’s public key and then encrypts the resulting ciphertext once again using her own private key and sends the resultant doubly encrypted message to B. B first uses A’s public key and, on the result, uses his own private key to get back the original message. Figure 3 illustrates this process. Asymmetric cipher ■This approach provides confidentiality. H can decrypt using A’s public key and undo the effect of A’s private key; but the result is still ciphertext created with B’s public key that requires B’s private key to decrypt. Not possessing B’s private key, H can go no further. ■This approach preserves integrity. The second step in the decryption process by B is to use A’s public key. This can work only on a message encrypted with A’s private key. Not having A’s private key, H cannot insert anything that will decrypt properly with A’s public key. I NFORMA T I ON SYST EMS SECUR I T Y 48 WWW. I NFOSECTODAY . COM ■This approach provides authentication. When B uses A’s public key followed by his own private key and gets anything meaningful, it is clear that the message was actually sent by A. No one other than A could have sent anything that could be decrypted meaningfully with A’s public key. Although this seems to be a complete solution, it leaves open two problems: Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 ■Encryption and decryption with asym metric key ciphers are extremely com puting intensive. The operations are slower by a factor of 100 when compared with symmetric key ciphers. Thus, the above scenario is actually impractical from a computing-time perspective. Asymmetric key ciphers are therefore seldom used for entire communications unless the amount of data being encrypted is very small. We will see later in the article that they are generally used only to exchange public keys spontane ously when needed, and to use these keys to agree on a symmetric session key. The session key is then used for the rest of the session with a symmetric key cipher. ■Communicating parties would need to manage large numbers of public keys, and if two unacquainted parties needed to communicate they would have to go through the ritual of public key exchange first — but how do they trust each other in the first place? Digital certificates and certificate authorities, discussed later, address this issue. Although the method of double encryp tion of the message does solve the integrity problem, we have seen that it is not practi cal. A practical approach is based on digital signatures. DIGITAL SIGNATURES To explain how digital signatures work, I first need to introduce the concept of mes sage digests. A message digest is a simple number that can be quickly calculated from an input message. A good message digest has the property that if even a single bit of the input message is changed, the digest cal culated will have a different value. Further, it is almost impossible to intentionally con struct two messages that have the same digest value. For the moment, assume that we are con cerned only about integrity and that there is no requirement for confidentiality. (I will bring confidentiality back into the equation later.) In such a case, when A sends a mes sage to B, A can send the message along with its calculated digest value. On receiv ing the message, B re-computes the digest value and compares it to the value sent by A. If the two match, B can be confident that the message was not changed in any way in transit; if the message was modified, the two message digest values would not match. It is easy to see that message digests alone are insufficient to thwart a hacker. After all, the hacker can interpose and replace the message with a new one and also replace the digest with the digest corre sponding to the new message. Thus, the recip ient will find that the digests match even though the message has lost its integrity. Digital signatures use the concept of message digests with an added twist based on asymmetric keys to completely thwart hackers from surreptitiously modifying a message in transit. Once again we assume that our only concern is integrity. Using dig ital signatures, when A sends a message to B, A first calculates the message digest. A then encrypts the message digest with her private key. The result is the digital signa ture of the message. A now sends the origi nal plaintext message along with its digital signature to B. Because the message digest is only a simple number, using asymmetric key ciphers on it is not computationally intensive. On receiving the message, B first uses A’s public key to decrypt the digital signa ture. This provides B with the message digest value. B can then independently gen erate the message digest from the plaintext message and compare the two. If they CRYP TOGRAPHY 49 J ANUARY / F EBRUARY 2006 FIGURE 4 Creating and Verifying Digital Signatures A’s private key plaintext Digest Algorithm message digest B’s computer A’s public key digital signature Asymmetric cipher Asymmetric cipher message digest 1 digital signature If the two digests match then B knows that the message is genuine Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 plaintext Digest Algorithm match, then B is confident that no one has tampered with the message. This process is illustrated in Figure 4. Under this scenario, it is impossible for the hacker (H) to undetectably replace the transmission with a new message and a matching digital signature because H does not have A’s private key. Suppose H does replace the message with a new message and replaces the digital signature with one generated using his own private key and then sends the message and the signature to B. B, assuming that the message is coming from A, will use A’s public key on the digi tal signature. This will fail to produce the actual message digest that H calculated because H did not use A’s private key. B will thus know that someone has tampered with the message. Digital signatures do not address the con fidentiality issue at all. They do solve the integrity problem completely. To a limited extent, they also solve the problem of authentication, if the message recipient has the public key of the sender. In real terms it is impractical to expect that any two com municants will have each other’s public keys readily available at all times. We need a scalable way of exchanging public keys spontaneously with a guarantee of the iden tities of the parties involved. This is where message digest 2 digital certificates and certificate authorities come into the picture. DIGITAL CERTIFICATES As before, let us assume that A and B have already exchanged public keys and trust each other. Suppose C now wants to have a secure communication with A, but A and C are not mutually acquainted. Unless A knows or trusts C, A will refuse to have a secure conversation with C. Suppose B knows and trusts C. Now, because A trusts B and B trusts C, there is a chain of trust potentially enabling A to trust C. How is this chain of trust established electroni cally? Because B trusts C, B can issue a dig ital certificate to C. A digital certificate consists of identifying information about an entity along with the signed public key of that entity. It essentially states that the issuer of the certificate believes that the public key in the certificate belongs to the person whose identifying information appears in the certificate. To issue a digital certificate to C, B cre ates a structure that contains identifying information about C (e.g., the name and other important public information) and attaches C’s public key encrypted with the private key of B. Because B is the only entity that can use B’s private key, the certificate I NFORMA T I ON SYST EMS SECUR I T Y 50 WWW. I NFOSECTODAY . COM FIGURE 5 B Creating a Digital Certificate for C B’s private key Digest Algorithm Information about B Information about C C’s public key Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 message digest essentially represents that B vouches for C’s identity, and anyone having B’s public key can verify the fact. This process is illus trated in Figure 5. The above arrangement works fine when there is an intermediary such as B who can establish a chain of trust between the com municating parties. In most business situa tions, especially in Web-based business-to consumer E-commerce, this requirement would be cumbersome. This issue is addressed by certificate authorities. CERTIFICATE AUTHORITIES Certificate authorities (CAs) are organiza tions specifically created to issue digital cer tificates after properly verifying the identities of entities requesting them. Many CAs exist; some well-known ones are Veri sign, Certisign, and Thawte. CAs are assumed to be trusted parties and hence the certificates issued by them serve to verify the identities of the parties presenting them. For example, in a Web-based E-commerce transaction, a buyer would want to ensure that she is really dealing with a genuine Web site and not that of an imposter before enter ing sensitive information such as a credit card number. The vendor can establish identity by presenting a digital certificate issued by a CA. The public keys of CAs are widely dis tributed. All Web browsers typically come Asymmetric cipher digital signature C’s digital certificate pre-installed with these public keys. Sup pose two parties A and C are unacquainted and one of them, say C, wishes to initiate a secure conversation with the other. C first gets a digital certificate from a CA and pre sents it to A. A already has the public key of well-known CAs and hence can use the appropriate one to extract C’s public key from the certificate. Similarly, A can present her certificate to C, from which C can get A’s public key. The above mecha nism can be employed to spontaneously exchange public keys with complete confi dence about the identities of the communi cating parties. However, as already mentioned, using public key encryption is computing inten sive and impractical for complete conversa tions. This final hurdle is addressed by Secure Sockets Layer. SECURE SOCKETS LAYER Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) was created by Netscape Corporation to address the diffi culty of using public key encryption for complete conversations. It provides a way to use a symmetric key cipher (much more efficient than asymmetric key ciphers) in a secure manner. It is specifically designed for Web-based E-commerce wherein a browser and server need to engage in a secure conversation. The following steps occur in an SSL-based exchange: CRYP TOGRAPHY 51 J ANUARY / F EBRUARY 2006 ■The server presents a digital certificate issued by a CA. ■The browser verifies the certificate and extracts the server’s public key. ■The browser then generates a symmetric key (called session key). ■The browser encrypts the session key with the server’s public key and sends it to the server. ■The server extracts the session key. ■The browser and server conduct a secret conversation using the shared session key, but they can use a symmetric key cipher for much greater efficiency. Downloaded By: [Canadian Research Knowledge Network] At: 21:03 30 August 2010 ■Server and browser discard the session key at the end of the conversation. In the above scenario, only the server is authenticated by means of a digital certifi cate; this is all that is typically needed for business-to-consumer E-commerce. A ver sion of SSL includes client authentication as well, which can be used when both parties need to be authenticated. When we see “https://——” in our browser’s address line, it indicates that SSL, 52 and therefore the full weight of public key cryptography, is in use. CONCLUSIONS This article has introduced the essential aspects of public key cryptography. We looked at the shortcomings of symmetric key ciphers used in isolation and showed how public key cryptography based on asymmetric key ciphers overcomes the problems by enabling confidentiality, mes sage integrity, and authentication. Finally, we looked at the essentials of SSL, which combines the power of public key cryptog raphy with the speed of symmetric key ciphers to provide security and effi ciency. References 1. Harold F. Tipton (Editor), Micki Krause (Editor), Information Security Management Handbook, Fifth Edition, Auerbach Publications, 2004. 2.
